      A RE L ANGUAGE M ODEL O UTPUTS
002   C ALIBRATED IN N UMERIC C ONTEXTS ?
003
004
005    Anonymous authors
006    Paper under double-blind review
007
008
009                                                               A BSTRACT
010
011                Some information is factual (e.g., “Paris is in France”), whereas other information
012                is probabilistic (e.g., “the coin flip will be a [Heads/T ails].”). We believe that
013                good Language Models (LMs) should understand and reflect this nuance. Our
014                work investigates this by testing if LMs’ output probabilities are calibrated to their
015                textual contexts. We define model “calibration“ as the degree to which the output
016                probabilities of candidate tokens are aligned with the relative likelihood that should
017
                   be inferred from the given context. For example, if the context concerns two equally
                   likely options (e.g., heads or tails for a fair coin), the output probabilities should
018
                   reflect this. Likewise, context that concerns non-uniformly likely events (e.g.,
019
                   rolling a six with a die) should also be appropriately captured with proportionate
020                output probabilities. We find that even in simple settings the best LMs (1) are poorly
021                calibrated, and (2) have systematic biases (e.g., preferred colors and sensitivities to
022                word orderings). For example, gpt-4o-mini often picks the first of two options
023                presented in the prompt regardless of the options’ implied likelihood, whereas
024                Llama-3.1-8B picks the second. Our other consistent finding is mode-collapse:
025                Instruction-tuned models often over-allocate probability mass on a single option.
026                These systematic biases introduce non-intuitive model behavior, making models
027                harder for users to understand.
028
029
030
      1        I NTRODUCTION
031
      We investigate the extent to which language model (LM) output probabilities are calibrated to the
032
      numeric content of their contexts. Consider the contexts below:
033
034
          (1)     From 17 blue marbles and 99 red marbles, Tommy reached blindly into a bag and grabbed a
035               marble that was the color [blue/red]
036
          (2)     From 98 blue marbles and 99 red marbles, Tommy reached blindly into a bag and grabbed a
037
                  marble that was the color [blue/red]
038
039
040    (1)       Problem                        Calibrated    GPT-4o         (2)       Problem                        Calibrated     GPT-4o
041               Setup                            Ideal     Outputs                    Setup                            Ideal      Outputs
                           Probability


                                                                                                 Probability


                    99                                                                 98 99                                            99.7%
042    100                               100%         85% 90.4%              100                               100%
       Numeric

                                                                             Context

045        0                              0%                                    0                               0%
                blue red                        blue red     blue red               blue red                          blue red      blue red
046
047   Figure 1: Models produce un-calibrated results. Inputting Examples 1 and 2 to gpt-4o different,
048   uncalibrated behaviors arise in the model probabilities. For (1), gpt-4o over-weights the first option
049   listed in the prompt, predicting blue with 90.4% when 15% is appropriate. For (2), gpt-4o over-
050   weights the option with a higher number beyond the calibrated ratio, predicting red with 99.7% when
051   50.2% is appropriate. We term the first behavior Pick First and the second Pick Higher.
052
053   We believe the probabilities for the next generated token should be calibrated to the relevant numeric
      content, capturing some of the nuance of probabilistic information. E.g., in the examples above, the


                                                                         1
054
      probability of an LM continuing with the bracketed tokens should be proportional to the respective
055   bolded values. In Example 1, the probability of blue should be approximately 15 th the probability of
056   red. We also evaluated how models behave when prompted to sample from distributions, like below:
057
058       (3)   Sampling from a uniform distribution with support from 2 inclusive to 5 exclusive, I sampled
059             the number [0/1/2/3 . . . 9]
060
061   In Example 3, the tokens 2, 3, 4 should each have a probability of 1/3, while other digits should have
062   probabilities close to 0. In other words, when the target output is based directly on probabilities
063   discernible from the context, randomness should arise from the model probabilities and the sampling
064   algorithm, rather than from arbitrary factors within the context.
065
066
            What Do We Mean By Calibration?
067        LMs output probabilities are calibrated to their contexts if the probabilities of relevant tokens
068        correspond to the numeric content implicitly or explicitly defined by their contexts. If tokens
069        t1 , t2 , . . . tn are indicated by context C to have probabilities P = p1 , p2 . . . pn , a calibrated
070        LM m outputs corresponding probabilities πi = m(ti |c):
071
                                                p i ∝ πi ,   i ∈ 1, 2 . . . n.
072
073        We measure calibration by the distance between P and Π = π1 , π2 , . . . πn , Section 3.2.
074
075   Stepping back, our motivation is drawn from the fact that while pre-training endows language models
076   with a wide range of capabilities (Brown et al., 2020a; Wei et al., 2022), further training can increase
077   the utility of existing models by encouraging them to follow instructions (Wei et al., 2021; Sanh et al.,
078
      2021) or match human preferences (Ouyang et al., 2022) rather than human behavior implicit in
      natural human text. Part of this project is ensuring that a model’s overt reasoning process aligns with
079
      its internal reasoning process (Lanham et al., 2023). This idea is also critical for model explanation
080
      methods (Chen et al., 2023): counterfactual simulatability requires that an explanation captures the
081   “reason“ a model arrived at an answer. Along the same lines, for contexts that call for contextual
082   calibration, e.g., situations where the context suggests probabilities over the next tokens, the most
083   direct solution is for models to yield exactly those probabilities. Kusner et al. (2017) also studies
084   probability differences in the setting of counterfactual fairness.
085
      There are large-scale efforts for models to not reproduce endemic online biases (Bai et al., 2022;
086
      Rudinger et al., 2018); we test if models are calibrated to the numeric content in context rather than
087   other patterns. Such biases are common with people, like the first-mentioned bias (Van Koevering
088   & Kleinberg, 2024) where the first option listed is favored. LLMs have also been shown to exhibit
089   biases concerning option order in multiple-choice questions (Pezeshkpour & Hruschka, 2023).
090
091         Contributions: Models Are Not Calibrated
092        Though different models have different error patterns, no model is well-calibrated. Moreover,
093        there are some shared themes across models and model families:
094        1. Mode-collapse: Instruction-tuning tends to improve the allocation of probability mass
095           upon valid options, but instruction-tuning also leads to decreased entropy in model outputs.
096           This can both improve (such as in Example 1) or reduce (such as in Example 3) calibration.
097
           2. Models have systematic tendencies: Many models show preferences for certain tokens
098           (such as numbers or colors), and the word order can also influence which tokens the
099           models favor.
100
101
102   2     R ELATED W ORK
103
104   Models struggle to calibrate internal uncertainty with textual outputs. A related but separate
105   line of work has studied the calibration in neural networks in terms of their ability to match their
106   prediction confidence with prediction accuracy: a network with 0.8 confidence on each prediction
107   should classify 80% of examples correctly (Guo et al., 2017; Minderer et al., 2021). Another line
      of work investigates how well the model outputs align with uncertainty (Yona et al., 2024; Kumar


                                                             2
108
      et al., 2024; Zhou et al., 2023; Lin et al., 2022), linguistic calibration (Mielke et al., 2022). Yona
109   et al. (2024) report that models struggle to exhibit their internal uncertainty with text, while Kumar
110   et al. (2024) measure how well model confidence is aligned internally (model probabilities) and
111   externally (via eliciting model to choose among a Likert scale.) In our work, we look at the calibration
112   from the perspective of agreement between the probability of predicted outputs and the contextually
113   defined likelihood of outputs in the text. We find that models often are certain when they should
114   be properly uncertain; mode-collapse arises both via supervised fine-tuning and reward-based fine-
115   tuning (O’Mahony et al., 2024; Janus, 2022). Zhou et al. (2024) likewise find that RLHF biases
116   models towards certainty, leading to an over-generation of strengtheners (e.g., “I am certain. . . ”) over
117
      weakeners (e.g., “Maybe. . . ”).
118
119   Recent work investigates if language models can output random results. Van Koevering &
120   Kleinberg (2024) shows that single coin flip predictions are biased towards heads and otherwise
121   demonstrate a first-mentioned bias. Silva (2024) shows that large models can 1) correctly describe
122   Matching Pennies, a game popular in game theory, and 2) code optimal behavior. Hopkins et al.
123   (2023), work closely related to our own, examines whether language models can induce uniform
124   distributions in the 0 to 1 interval.
125
126
127
      3     E XPERIMENTAL D ESIGN
128
129   We run two experiments that evaluate if model probabilities are calibrated to the numeric values either
130   explicit or implicit in context, Section 3.4 (Distributions) and Section 3.5 (Probabilities). See
131
      Appendix appendix A for more information on reproducing these experiments.
132
133   3.1   P ROBLEM S ETUP
134
135   Each problem assumes a context C to be continued by a relevant token among t1 , t2 , . . . tn = T ⊂ V
136   where V is the full vocabulary. Each token ti is associated with a probability pi forming a distribution,
137   P = p1 , p2 , . . . pn . In Example 1, T is {red, blue} with P = {red : 0.75, blue : 0.25}. For a
138   model m, we define its probabilities over these words as Π = π1 , π2 , . . . where πi ≈ m(ti |C), with
139   one addendum. We sum the probabilities
                                         P        of all tokenizations for a given word, which can include
140   capitalization and spaces: πi = s∈Tokenizations(ti ) m(s|C). E.g., we sum probabilities for “red”,
141   “Red”, “_red”, and “_Red.”
142
143   3.2   M ETRICS AND E VALUATION
144
145   Our primary metrics below help us ask how well a model calibrates to numeric information in context.
146
      Probability Mass (PM): Calibration, the relative probability        mass on tokens in T, only matters
147                                                          . P
      if the PM that falls upon T is sufficient, PM(T ) = t : T πt . If PM(T ) for a model is low (say,
148
      0.30), this indicates that the model is not capturing the intended relationship between the context and
149
      T . However, when PM is high (say, 0.75), we can start to meaningfully ask questions about how
150   probability mass is allocated among, say, red and blue. While our metric for measuring calibration
151   (introduced below) would also capture when the PM is too low, separately measuring this information
152   makes it easier to understand and compare model behavior.
153
      Relative Entropy (RE): To detect mode collapse, we measure RE, the ratio between entropy H of
154
      model probabilities and the calibrated probabilities, H(Π)/H(P ). An RE less than one implies that
155
      the probability mass is overly concentrated.
156
157   Wasserstein Distance (WD): To measure calibration, we use WD. WD (Kantorovich, 1939) captures
158   the movement between one distribution (or set of values) and another.1
159
160       1
            Kullback–Leibler (KL) divergence is also a natural choice. However, a number of the target distributions
161   we have include 0 values where KL would be undefined. We also compute Mean-Squared Error (MSE) but don’t
      find a material difference in the findings so only report the WD.


                                                            3
162
      3.3     M ODELS
163
164   We test open-source models that have both base and instruction-tuned versions: Mistral-7B-v0.1,
165   Mistral-7B-v0.3, Mixtral-8x7B-v0.1, Yi-1.5-9B, Yi-1.5-34B, Meta-Llama-3.1-8B,
166   gemma-2-9b, gemma-2-27b (Jiang et al., 2023; 2024; Young et al., 2024; Dubey et al., 2024; Team
167   et al., 2024); and four proprietary models, gpt-3.5, gpt-4-turbo, gpt-4o-mini, and gpt-4o
168   (Brown et al., 2020b; Achiam et al., 2023; OpenAI, 2024). We prompt instruction-tuned models
169   to be calibrated. We refer to the base, unprompted version of the models as “base” and the
170
      instruction-tuned, prompted models as “chat.” Model keys are listed in Appendix Table 4.
171
172   3.4     E XPERIMENT: Distributions
173
      This experiment tests if models can produce a uniform distribution in their output probabilities.
174
      Example 3, repeated below, is representative of the instances in this dataset:
175
176
        (3)    Sampling from a uniform distribution with support from 2 inclusive to 5 exclusive, I sampled
177            the number [0/1/2/3 . . . 9]
178
179   We test across different numeric ranges, range inclusivities, and templates. The numbers scale from
180   10 to 903 and we use five different templates; there are 4500 unique instances. In some of our
181   analyses, we group problem ranges and inclusivities that support the same tokens: For example, [12,
182   15) and [92, 95) are grouped. There are 615 such groups.
183
      From here, the question is if the distribution of the next token is calibrated to the uniform dis-
184
      tribution ΠC = U(2, 5). As noted above, we report WD, which for this example would be:
185   WD(ΠT , U(2, 5)) = WD(ΠT , [0, 0, 1/3, 1/3, 1/3, 0, . . . , 0], where T includes digits (tokens) 0 to
186   9 and U(2, 5) covers all digits (with 0 for unsupported digits).
187
188   Additional Metrics. We introduce three metrics to understand if there are patterns in the mode.
189
190
      Mode Probability is the maximum probability of Π averaged across all examples in the dataset,
      AVGdataset max Π.
191
192   Mode Stability is the rate at which the most likely token is preserved between base and chat
193   versions of a model averaged across all examples in the dataset, AVGdataset (1(arg max Πbase =
194   arg max Πchat )).
195   Mode Frequency examines whether there are biases for particular tokens (numbers). This metric
196   measures the frequency of the mode averaged across distributions. Because different distributions
197   cover different digits, we group the distributions by underlying problem range and inclusivity: E.g.,
198   [2, 5) and [132, 135) are grouped. This is equivalent to: (1) setting the temperature to 0 (greedy
199   sampling), (2) averaging over the groups, keeping the digits dimension, (3) taking the maximum for
200   each group, and (4) averaging across all maximums, AVG(maxdigits AVGdistributions, keepdim (Πτ =0 )).
201
202   3.5     E XPERIMENT: Probabilities
203
204
      This experiment tests whether the models’ output probabilities reflect values in their context.2 The
      following is a representative example of the dataset:
205
206
207
        (4)    From 17 red marbles and 99 blue marbles, Billy reached blindly into the bag and grabbed a
               marble with the color [red/blue]
208
209
210
      We use five templates, three numeric scales with 100 configurations each, and 110 pairs of options
      (e.g., red/blue, or orange/purple). The numeric scales are (1) all numbers under ten, (2) ten numbers
211
      sampled under 100, and (3) ten numbers sampled under 1000. We these settings bias model behavior.
212
      Some factors, such as the numeric context, should influence the model’s behavior, while others, such
213   as the ordering of the options, should not.
214
          2
215         Probabilities tests the basic rules of probabilities. As models improve, tests that capture a wider range
      of probabilities rules would be exciting: Bayes theorem, De Morgan’s Laws, Event Independence, etc.


                                                            4
216
      To simplify the analysis of this large number of configurations, we categorize the model behavior
217   into one of six potential reference points based on WD. In practice, we find that models typically
218   follow one of these six behaviors, making this a useful summary of performance.
219
220
      Baselines and Reference Points First, we create Random baselines to contextualize the metric
221   values. Each Randomτ baseline: For every row in the dataset, the random baseline produces a Π by
222   uniformly sampling two random numbers from 0 to 1 which are then transformed into a distribution
223   parameterized by a temperature τ using the softmax function. Second, we observe several behaviors
224   in the results; we create a baseline for each of these behaviors (listed below).
225
226          0. Null: The PM is too low. This can happen for smaller models like Mistral-7B-v0.1.
227          1. Calibrated: Sets Π = P . This is the best case.
228       2, 3. Pick Higher: Sets all probability mass on the arg max Π. This baseline is calibrated
229             directionally: The option with higher probability is correctly preferred but not proportionally.
230             It could alternatively be viewed as mode-collapse. Instead of placing all probability mass
231             on the arg max Π, Pick Higherp=q places q (say, 0.7) on arg max and 1 − q on arg min
232             (0.3). In Appendix Table 6, we show performance across a wide range of p. (Pick Lower
233             analogously places all probability mass on the arg min Π, this rarely occurs.)
234       4, 5. Pick First: Sets all probability mass on the first option, ignoring numeric information.
235             Pick Second: analogously places all probability mass on the second option.
236
237
      4     R ESULTS
238
239
      4.1   Distributions
240
241   There are a few patterns across all models. Table 1 (left) reports the primary metrics. Chat (instruction-
242   tuned) models are less calibrated and have a lower RE than their base counterparts. The base models
243   are more calibrated than the Random baselines. All tested gpt-* models have similar calibration
244   scores to the open-source chat models.
245
      We find evidence that the reduced performance of chat models results from their tendency to over-
246   allocate probability on a subset of valid tokens. Table 1 (right) provides additional metrics that
247   suggest mode-collapse. For chat models, the most likely token receives, on average, 66% of the
248   probability mass (up from 28% for base models, beyond the empirical ideal 23%, on this dataset).
249   The most likely token remains the same for 48% instances across base and chat models, suggesting
250   that the mode is often preserved.
251

268   the minimum mode frequency would be the same as the rate of the uniform distribution; we see
269   much higher rates, ranging from +29 to +50%. The implication is that models are also uncalibrated
      at an outcome level with systematic biases (preferences) for certain numbers. To give an example,

      Mass (PM) scores are high for base and chat models, but calibration scores (WD) are lower (better) for

             Distributions: Systematic Behaviors
301
302         (1) Chat models place a majority of probability mass on a subset of valid tokens;
303         (2) The mode is often stable between base to chat models at the instance level.
304         (3) We see poor calibration at the instance level and disproportionate outcomes.

      4.2     Probabilities
307
308   High-level results. All models perform worse than some simple baselines. Randomτ =1.0 performs
309   better than all models. Pick higherp=0.7 , which allocates 0.7 probability mass to the option associated
310   with the higher value, further outperforms all models. Still, we see interesting performance differences
311   across models.
312
      There are two key parallels between the results in this set of experiments and those in the last section:
313   (1) Instruction tuning decreases the entropy of model output probabilities. The decrease in entropy
314   is often due to a large increase of probability on the token with the corresponding higher value
315   (replicating Pick Higher). Unlike the previous experiment, this tends to improve the calibration
316   score overall. Table 2 displays high-level results. (2) Model behavior is systematic (and uncalibrated).
317   Understanding (2) requires disaggregating the results and examining model behavior across different
318   colors and color orderings.
319   Further Analysis. To make headway, we characterize the results of two models (Llama-3.1-8B
320   and gpt-4o-mini) in one setting in Figure 3. The full spread of results is available in Appendix C.2.
321   Here, our aim is a high-level understanding of which behaviors best characterize these models. The
322
          3
323         By looking at Appendix Figure 11, for gemma-2-27b (chat), we can see that the average probability on 3 for
      [0, 7) is 0.75, also far beyond expectation.

      Figure 3 shows that model behavior can be dependent on word ordering and that there is a hierarchy

395

      first color is Pick-ed First, whereas when the order is flipped then there is Pick Higher. In this
396
      particular example, orange/pink/purple “dominate” most other colors and white is “dominated” by all
397
      other colors. This step-wise behavior was neither required nor expected.

423
424   5             H UMAN S TUDY
425
426   Our experiments so far have demonstrated that these models are generally poorly calibrated. In this
427   section, we try to provide greater context for these results by directly comparing the calibration
428   of proprietary models against humans. We do so using a variation of the storied game, Matching
429   Pennies. The only foolproof approach to winning this game is to behave as randomly as possible.
430   Results are presented in Table 3. The two main takeaways are: 1) Models also produce non-random
431   sequences in this setup, further demonstrating their lack of calibration; 2) When prompted, the
      model’s performance improves and is on par with human performance.


                                                                               8
432
      5.1      E XPERIMENTAL D ESIGN
433
434   This experiment is based on a simple heads/tails game. Our motivation for including this experiment
435   is to allow a human baseline in a setting where random behavior is optimal. This section makes no
436   distinction between calibrated probabilities and unbiased outputs because we don’t have access to
437   this information for humans. However, to “perform” well, or appear calibrated, requires either the
438   model/person to (1) produce calibrated probabilities which when sampled from will yield random
439   outputs, (2) use an internal random process to produce uncalibrated probabilities that yield random
440   outputs, or (3) have a systematic process for producing outputs that appear random enough. (Previous
441
      work would suggest that humans are limited in this regard and will likely not be random (and therefore
      not calibrated).) This leaves us with a one-sided problem. If the humans/models were proficient at
442
      producing random outcomes we would have to adjudicate between the above possibilities.
443
444   We focus on a variation of the game similar to Eliaz & Rubinstein (2011). Each round, Player 1 aims
445   to match her chosen coin face with Player 2’s choice, getting a +1 reward if their coin faces match,
446   and -1 otherwise. In a single-round game, the Nash Equilibrium is to select a coin face randomly.
447
      We set there to be 100 rounds of play. To focus on calibration, we frame the tested model as the
      “misleader.” The misleader is prompted to submit answers, and the “guesser” is set to predict the
448
      misleader’s answers. The misleader submits m1 , m2 , . . . m100 answers, and the guesser bases each
449
      of its gi answers causally based upon all previous misleader’s answers, {mj | 0 < j < i}. We use a
450
      n-gram4 based strategy as our guesser.
451
452
453   Models We focus on gpt-* models, sampling temperature τ = 1, and compare them to human
454
      behavior. Human behavior is drawn from a sample of 44 games of 100 rounds from n = 10 different
      volunteers with technical backgrounds across up to t < 10 different trials.
455
456
457   Additional Metrics Whereas previous metrics rely on model probabilities, this experiment assumes
458   access only to outputs, meaning we cannot use the metrics used in previous sections.
459   Player Win Rate (PWR) is the rate the misleader defeats the guesser, averaging over 100 games
460   each for up to 100 rounds. The higher this rate, the more random the outputs of the misleader, and
461   thus the more calibrated it is at an outcome level.
462
      Randomness Testing (RNG). The Wald–Wolfowitz5 runs test checks if the elements of a sequence
463   are mutually independent, and can often rule out human-generated sentences as non-random. Using
464   this test at a significance level of 0.05, we report the proportion of samples that cannot statistically
465   be rejected as non-random (note this does not guarantee them to be random) and label this value as
466   RNG. For reference, as shown in Table 3 when we sampled random sequences using python, 98%
467   of the sequences could not be rejected; only 7% of human-generated samples could not be rejected.
468
469
      5.2      R ESULTS
470
471   When a model is instructed to be random (denoted by “+instr”) Player Win Rate (PWR) improves. For
472   gpt-4o, PWR increases by +30%. Explicit instruction following ability can thus improve calibration
473   at the outcome level. Notably, when prompted, model performance is similar to human performance.
474
      Table 3 reports the proportion of samples (where each sample is 100 coin flips), at p = 0.05, the
475
      result could not be considered non-random: Higher RNG values suggest the model is more random.
476   More model- than human-generated samples were considered random, 7% vs 19 − 43%.
477
478          Humans and Proprietary (OpenAI) Models Are Both Non-Random (Uncalibrated)
479
            (1) Even top-performing proprietary models are unable to produce random behavior
480
            (2) Human behavior is also non-random. Even if models are currently similar to humans in
481
            this regard, we argue that future work endowing models with the ability to produce calibrated
482         outputs would improve human interactions with models.
483
484
         4
485          Motivated by the strategy here: https://www.expunctis.com/2019/03/07/Not-so-random.html.
         5
             Results with Bartel’s test perfectly correlate with the runs test so we only report the runs test.


                                                             9
486
                                                     PWR↑                 RNG↑
487
                                                  base +instr.         base +instr.
488
489                                Random                0.50                 0.98
490
                                   Human                 0.33                 0.07
491                                gpt-3.5        0.26          0.40   0.31          0.43
492                                gpt-4-turbo    0.02          0.33   0.00          0.25
493                                gpt-4o         0.06          0.36   0.08          0.19
494
495   Table 3: Human Study Results: Player win rates (PWR) and statistical tests for randomness
496   (RNG). Higher values for both metrics suggest that models are more able to produce random (and
497
      thus more calibrated) outputs. Takeaways: (1) gpt-3.5 stands out as the most random model; (2)
      Models pass a greater number of the statistical tests for randomness than humans, though are still far
498
      from random; (3) Model sequences are predictable at rates similar to humans; (4) Adding instructions
499
      to be random leads to more random outputs.
500
501
502   6    D ISCUSSION
503
504   Why Aren’t LLMs Already Calibrated? Should the negative log-likelihood loss endow a pre-
505   trained language model with calibration? If there were n → ∞ examples of Heads and Tails being
506   flipped in the data and the true distribution was reflective of some pheads , then the lowest loss would
507   be achieved via a perfectly calibrated model. Thus, the answer is empirical: What does the training
508
      data look like? Finding the disparate scenarios that call for random outcomes may be difficult.
      Non-calibrated outputs frequently occurring in such scenarios might harm numeric calibration. For
509
      example, patterns derived from people preferring the number 7 (out of 10) or 42 (out of 100) or Heads
510
      (over Tails) or answering with round numbers ending in 0 or 5 might reduce calibration. Possibly,
511   some models are capable of producing calibrated results; the models may “know” the calibrated
512   output but instead match non-random tendencies, akin to pragmatics.
513
514   Should Models Be Calibrated? Our work highlights how models that perform remarkably in
515   other types of evaluations fall short in scenarios that call for calibrated outputs. Some may have
516   expected our results given that these models are trained on data generated by people who are also
517   widely biased. Others might have expected that with the range of data seen in training (data in the
518   wild, code outputs, endemic instances of distributions) models would learn to calibrate. However, it
519   seems curious that models are frequently able to correctly generate text describing what the proper
520   calibrated distribution should be for a given scenario, but fail to represent that distribution internally
521
      when prompted to simulate the same scenario.
522
      Can We Calibrate Models with FineTuning or using Tools? We expect that it is possible to
523
      fine-tune models to improve calibration. Already we see some improvements with generic alignment
524
      procedures, though these improvements often come at a tradeoff of an (overly large) reduction in
525   model entropy. Mitigating this mode collapse behavior is a compelling area for future work. Using
526   tools (Schick et al., 2024) is another practical solution for simple scenarios like those we present, but
527   may not obviously generalize to more complex scenarios.
528
529
530
      7    C ONCLUSION
531
      All models we test are not only not calibrated, but behave in different systematically uncalibrated
532
      ways. A common theme is that top-performing models (e.g., gemma-2-27b and gpt-* models) Pick
533
      Higher, allocating most of the probability mass upon single output token. This is an example of
534   mode collapse. Though mode-collapse is commonly studied in computer vision and image generation
535   (Thanh-Tung & Tran, 2020; Srivastava et al., 2017) inter alia, O’Mahony et al. (2024) show how
536   RLHF reduces diversity in model outputs, leading to this problem in text generation. Our results are
537   similar: Instruction-tuned models produce valid outputs, but the probability distribution is collapsed
538   to a small portion of the valid answers that are equally correct. While we focus on controlled problem
539   settings, these issues leave vulnerabilities. For instance, an adversary could leverage that a model is
      going to behave in a systematically arbitrary manner.

721   Mode preservation and frequency results are in Figures 7 to 11, extending the results shown in

731   Figure 16 shows model color preferences (or differences) across all models; the regularity of the
732
      results seen is striking. This figure shows that models will consistently be more calibrated for some
      colors over others; exactly how this translates into model behavior requires more nuance.

734   Figures 19 to 30 show this nuance with in-depth heatmap performances for all models across all
735   templates (columns) and numeric scales (grids). The legend for these latter plots is shown in Figure 17.
736   This set of results is extremely long. We provide a (Space Station) view of these results in Figure 18
737   which shows grouped and compressed figures. Only the general trends as shown by the cell colors
738
      can be discerned. What we can see is that the primary takeaways from this set of figures is that 1) for
      a given model, there is a lot of stability across numeric scales (10s, 100s, etc.) (columns per model
739
      are similar) and somewhat less stability across different templates/prompts (rows per model can
740
      differ). 2) Models tend to have different patterns of results from each other. 3) Pick first, pick higher
741   are most common followed by pick second. Many but not all models have obvious cross-diagonal
742   asymmetries.

815
      CoinFace = str, H: CoinFace = "H", T: CoinFace = "T"
      def flip(face: CoinFace) -> CoinFace:
816       return {H: T, T: H}[face]
817   def mdp_1gram(sequence: List[CoinFace]) -> CoinFace:
818       return _mdp_ngram(sequence, 1)
819   def mdp_2gram(sequence: List[CoinFace]) -> CoinFace:
820
          return _mdp_ngram(sequence, 2)
      def mdp_3gram(sequence: List[CoinFace]) -> CoinFace:
821       return _mdp_ngram(sequence, 3)
822   def mdp_4gram(sequence: List[CoinFace]) -> CoinFace:
823       return _mdp_ngram(sequence, 4)
824   def mdp_5gram(sequence: List[CoinFace]) -> CoinFace:
825
          return _mdp_ngram(sequence, 5)
      def _mdp_ngram(sequence: List[CoinFace], ngram_size: int) -> CoinFace:
826       """Picks the most common continuation for a given sequence based upon all n_grams
827       ,→ of the current size.
828
829      Defaults to H if sequence is empty. Flips previous coinface if the length of the
830
         ,→ sequence is 1.
         """
831      assert ngram_size >= 1
832
833      # Handle short sequences
834      if len(sequence) == 0:
835
             return H
         elif len(sequence) == 1:
836          if sequence[0] == H:
837              return T
838          else:
839              return H
840
         if len(sequence) <= ngram_size:
             return _mdp_ngram(sequence, min(ngram_size - 1, len(sequence) - 1))
841
842      map_ = {H: 0, T: 1}
843      sequence_numbers = [map_[face] for face in sequence]
844      mdp = np.zeros([2 for _ in range(ngram_size + 1)])
845
         for i in range(0, len(sequence_numbers) - ngram_size):
             index = tuple(sequence_numbers[i : i + (ngram_size + 1)])
846          mdp[index] = mdp[index] + 1
847
848      last = tuple(sequence_numbers[-ngram_size:])
849      transitions = mdp[last]
850
         if transitions[0] > transitions[1]:
             return H
851      elif transitions[0] == transitions[1]:
852          # NOTE: Alt, we could pick which ever is more common
853          # or fall back to a lower ngram or sample.
854          return H
855
         else:
             return T
856
857
      n-gram model implementation

1340   Figure 16: Model Color Preferences: Differences in model calibration scores when one color
1341   is first vs another. Higher scores indicate that the “winning” color, shown on the x-axis, has a
1342   comparatively better fit when listed first versus second. Takeaways: (1) Almost all models form
1343   hierarchies across colors where certain colors always lead to better fits when listed first versus second.
1344   (Note) This does not mean that any given color is picked more than other colors, just that there is
1345   an order bias where certain colors lead to more calibrated behaviors. When we further expand out
1346
       these results like into the heatmaps in the main body of the paper, we can better understand what the
       model behavior actually looks like. Because the ordering of the colors is different across all models,
1347
       without much shared structure, we do not have an obvious hypothesis about why these particular
1348
       color hierarchies arose.
